\begin{itemize}

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.

<score>S</score>

Let's analyze the game as a kind of "alternating resource" problem. Define the cumulative sums after each turn. The constraints: For odd n, S_n ≤ λ n; for even n, Q_n ≤ n.

We can try to find necessary and sufficient conditions for the existence of a sequence that avoids losing. This is like a "safety" condition. Perhaps we can find a "potential" that can be maintained by both players.

We can consider the following: Suppose we define a function f(n) = something like S_n^2 - n Q_n? Not sure.

Alternatively, we can consider that the game is similar to a "tug-of-war" where each player can spend their budget to increase the opponent's cumulative sum or square sum. Perhaps we can use a "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategy.

Let's try to understand the dynamics more abstractly.

Let’s denote after turn n, we have S_n and Q_n. The next turn is n+1. The player (Alice if n+1 odd, Bazza if even) must choose x_{n+1} ≥ 0 such that the relevant inequality holds. The player can always choose x_{n+1} = 0 if the inequality is already satisfied (i.e., S_n ≤ λ (n+1) for Alice, Q_n ≤ n+1 for Bazza). If the inequality is already violated, the player loses.

Thus, the game is about whether the state (S_n, Q_n) is "safe" for the next player. So the players can influence the state.

We can think in terms of "safe" sets: For each n, the set of (S, Q) such that both players can move from that state given the parity? But it's a game of alternating moves.

Perhaps we can model this as a "pursuit-evasion" game where each player wants to force the opponent into a region where the cumulative condition is violated.

Another approach: Since the constraints are linear in S and quadratic in Q, we can try to find the maximum possible S_n given that Q_n is at most n (if n even) or something. But the game is infinite.

Consider the following strategy for Alice: She wants to make Q_n > n+1 at some odd n. She can try to concentrate the sum into her own moves. The maximum Q_n she can achieve at odd n given that she must keep S_n ≤ λ n is S_n^2 (by putting all mass in one variable). But she also has to respect that Bazza's moves might increase Q. Actually, she can set her x_n as large as possible, i.e., λ n - S_{n-1}. But she also might want to set previous numbers to be small to allow room for her x_n. However, she controls her own choices; she can set them to maximize the square sum. Let's try to see if she can force a win for λ > 1.

Let's check λ=1.05. λ=1.05, λ^2=1.1025, sqrt(2-1.1025)=sqrt(0.8975)=0.947, x_3 = 2.1-0.947=1.153, x_3^2=1.329, Q_3=2+1.329=3.329 <4, so win? Actually, need Q_3 > 4 to win at n=4. The maximum Q_n she can achieve at odd n is S_n^2, where S_n is the sum up to n. She can try to make S_n as large as possible, i.e., λ n. Then Q_n ≤ λ^2 n^2. To have Q_n > n+1, we need λ^2 n^2 > n+1 => λ^2 n > 1 + 1/n => for large n, λ^2 n > 1, so λ > 0. So that's not a strong condition. But she needs to achieve that Q_n while also respecting that Bazza's moves may increase Q. However, she can choose her numbers to be large and Bazza's numbers to be small? Bazza will try to limit her ability to build up Q_n. So we need to analyze the game more carefully.

We can think of this as a two-player game where each player can "spend" their budget to increase the opponent's cumulative sum or square sum. Perhaps we can find a "critical" λ where the game becomes a draw, and then for larger λ, Alice wins; for smaller λ, Bazza wins.

From the simulations with λ=0.7, we found Bazza wins by simply setting x_2=sqrt(2), x_4=sqrt(2), ... and Alice setting zero. For λ=0.71, that strategy does not win because S_{2k} < (2k+1)λ for all k. But could Bazza use a different strategy to win for λ=0.71? For instance, he might try to set x_2 = c, then later set x_4 = sqrt(4 - c^2) (since Q_3 = c^2 if Alice sets x_3=0). Let's find the maximum possible S_4 given that Alice will choose x_1 to minimize S_4? She can also choose x_3 to limit. Actually, Bazza chooses x_2 and x_4; Alice chooses x_1 and x_3. The constraint: Q_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 ≤ 2, and Q_4 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 + x_3^2 + x_4^2 ≤ 4. Also, S_4 = x_1 + x_2 + x_3 + x_4. Bazza wants to maximize S_4; Alice wants to minimize S_4. This is a simple two-stage game. We can find the outcome assuming both play optimally. For λ=0.71, we want to see if Bazza can force S_4 > 5*0.71=3.55? Actually, at n=5 (odd), the condition is S_4 ≤ 5λ = 3.55. So if he can make S_4 > 3.55, then Alice loses at n=5. But is that possible? Let's try to find the maximum possible S_4 given that Alice can choose x_1 and x_3 to minimize it, while Bazza chooses x_2 and x_4 to maximize it, subject to Q_2 ≤ 2 and Q_4 ≤ 4.

We can consider that Bazza's best is to set x_2 = sqrt(2) if x_1=0, and x_4 = sqrt(4 - (x_1^2+x_2^2))? Actually, Q_2 = x_1^2+x_2^2 ≤ 2. For given x_1, the maximum x_2 is sqrt(2 - x_1^2). Then at n=3, Alice chooses x_3. She wants to minimize S_4, so she will set x_3=0. Then Q_3 = x_1^2+x_2^2 = 2 (if x_2 is max). Then at n=4, Bazza can set x_4 = sqrt(4 - Q_3) = sqrt(2). So S_4 = x_1 + sqrt(2 - x_1^2) + sqrt(2). Now, Alice can choose x_1 to minimize this expression. Let's find the minimum of f(x) = x + sqrt(2 - x^2) + sqrt(2) for x in [0, sqrt(2)]. f(x) = x + sqrt(2 - x^2) + 1.414. The function g(x)=x+sqrt(2-x^2) has minimum at x = ? Derivative: 1 + (1/(2 sqrt(2-x^2)))(-2x) = 1 - x/ sqrt(2-x^2). Set =0 => x = sqrt(2-x^2) => x^2 = 2-x^2 => 2x^2=2 => x=1. So minimum at x=1, g(1)=1+1=2. So minimum f = 2 + 1.414 = 3.414. So the maximum S_4 that Bazza can force given Alice plays optimally is 3.414. That is less than 3.55. So Alice survives at n=5. So for λ=0.71, Bazza cannot win at n=5. Could he win at a later turn?
